<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Type of salt-cured pork</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the meat. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Bacon_(disambiguation)" title="Bacon (disambiguation)">Bacon (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox hrecipe adr"><caption class="infobox-title fn"><span>Bacon</span></caption><tbody><tr><td class="infobox-image" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Made20bacon.png"><img alt="Made20bacon.png" data-file-height="382" data-file-width="698" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Made20bacon.png/220px-Made20bacon.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Made20bacon.png/330px-Made20bacon.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Made20bacon.png/440px-Made20bacon.png 2x" width="220"/></a><div class="infobox-caption" style="padding-bottom:0.25em;border-bottom:1px solid #aaa;">A strip of cooked side (streaky) bacon</div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="line-height:1.15em; padding-right:0.65em;">Type</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Curing_(food_preservation)" title="Curing (food preservation)">Cured</a> <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">pork</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="line-height:1.15em; padding-right:0.65em;">Main ingredients</th><td class="infobox-data ingredient"><a href="/wiki/Curing_(food_preservation)" title="Curing (food preservation)">Salt-cured</a> <a href="/wiki/Pork_belly" title="Pork belly">pork belly</a></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-below" colspan="2" style="border-top:1px solid #aaa;padding-top:0.25em;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1126788409">.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul{line-height:inherit;list-style:none;margin:0;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol li,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul li{margin-bottom:0}</style><div class="plainlist"><ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/16px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/24px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/32px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Cookbook:Bacon" title="b:Cookbook:Bacon">Cookbook: Bacon</a></li><li> <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Commons-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1376" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/12px-Commons-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/18px-Commons-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/24px-Commons-logo.svg.png 2x" width="12"/></a> <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Bacon" title="commons:Bacon">Media: Bacon</a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Bacon</b> is a type of <a href="/wiki/Curing_(food_preservation)" title="Curing (food preservation)">salt-cured</a> <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">pork</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Spruce_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Spruce-1">[1]</a></sup> made from various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuts_of_meat" title="Cuts of meat">cuts</a>, typically the <a href="/wiki/Pork_belly" title="Pork belly">belly</a> or less fatty parts of the back. It is eaten as a side dish (particularly in <a href="/wiki/Breakfast" title="Breakfast">breakfasts</a>), used as a central ingredient (e.g., the <a href="/wiki/BLT" title="BLT">bacon, lettuce, and tomato sandwich (BLT)</a>), or as a flavouring or accent (as in bacon bits in a salad).
</p><p>Bacon is also used for <a href="#Bacon_fat">barding</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Larding" title="Larding">larding</a> roasts, especially game, including <a href="/wiki/Venison" title="Venison">venison</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pheasant" title="Pheasant">pheasant</a>, and may also be used to insulate or flavour roast joints by being layered onto the meat. The word is derived from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a> <i>*bakkon</i>, meaning "back meat".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" bacon</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:211px;max-width:211px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bacon_in_a_pan_(cooked).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2180" data-file-width="2177" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Bacon_in_a_pan_%28cooked%29.jpg/209px-Bacon_in_a_pan_%28cooked%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Bacon_in_a_pan_%28cooked%29.jpg/314px-Bacon_in_a_pan_%28cooked%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Bacon_in_a_pan_%28cooked%29.jpg/418px-Bacon_in_a_pan_%28cooked%29.jpg 2x" width="209"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">After being pan-fried</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Meat from other animals, such as <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lamb_and_mutton" title="Lamb and mutton">lamb</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicken_(food)" title="Chicken (food)">chicken</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goat_meat" title="Goat meat">goat</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkey_meat" title="Turkey meat">turkey</a>, may also be cut, cured, or otherwise prepared to resemble bacon, and may even be referred to as, for example, "<a href="/wiki/Turkey_bacon" title="Turkey bacon">turkey bacon</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Such use is common in areas with significant <a href="/wiki/Kashrut" title="Kashrut">Jewish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_dietary_laws" title="Islamic dietary laws">Muslim</a> populations as both religions prohibit the consumption of pork.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vegetarian_bacon" title="Vegetarian bacon">Vegetarian bacons</a> such as "soy bacon" also exist.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc"/>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Curing_and_smoking">Curing and smoking</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Schweinebauch-1.jpg"><img class="thumbimage" data-file-height="777" data-file-width="1613" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Schweinebauch-1.jpg/220px-Schweinebauch-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Schweinebauch-1.jpg/330px-Schweinebauch-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Schweinebauch-1.jpg/440px-Schweinebauch-1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Schweinebauch-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Uncured pork belly</div></div></div>
<p>Before the advent of cheap and widespread artificial refrigeration in the modern era, the <a href="/wiki/Curing_(food_preservation)" title="Curing (food preservation)">curing</a> of meat was necessary for its safe long-term preservation. However, both the flavour imparted to the meat in doing so and the extended <a href="/wiki/Shelf_life" title="Shelf life">shelf life</a> it offered had become much prized, and although curing is in general no longer necessary in the developed world, it continues in wide use.
</p><p>Bacon is cured through either a process of injecting it with or soaking it in <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a>, known as wet curing, or rubbed with salt, known as dry curing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Spruce_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Spruce-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jwb_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-jwb-5">[5]</a></sup> Bacon brine has added curing ingredients, most notably <a href="/wiki/Nitrite" title="Nitrite">nitrites</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrates</a>, which speed the curing and stabilize colour. Cured bacon may then be dried for weeks or months in cold air, or it may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smoking_(food)" title="Smoking (food)">smoked</a> or <a href="/wiki/Boiling" title="Boiling">boiled</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Spruce_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-The_Spruce-1">[1]</a></sup> Fresh and dried bacon are typically cooked before eating, often by <a href="/wiki/Pan_frying" title="Pan frying">pan frying</a>. Boiled bacon is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ready-to-eat_food" title="Ready-to-eat food">ready to eat</a>, as is some smoked bacon, but they may be cooked further before eating. Differing flavours can be achieved by using various types of wood, or less common fuels such as <a href="/wiki/Corncob" title="Corncob">corn cobs</a> or <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a>. This process can take up to eighteen hours, depending on the intensity of the flavour desired. <i>The Virginia Housewife</i> (1824), thought to be one of the earliest American cookbooks, gives no indication that bacon is ever <i>not</i> smoked, though it gives no advice on flavouring, noting only that care should be taken lest the fire get too hot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacon is distinguished from other salt-cured <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">pork</a> by differences in the cuts of meat used and in the brine or dry packing. Historically, the terms "<a href="/wiki/Ham" title="Ham">ham</a>" and "bacon" referred to different cuts of meat that were brined or packed identically, often together in the same barrel. Today, ham is defined as coming from the hind portion of the pig and brine specifically for curing ham includes a greater amount of sugar, while bacon is less sweet, though ingredients such as <a href="/wiki/Brown_sugar" title="Brown sugar">brown sugar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Maple_syrup" title="Maple syrup">maple syrup</a> are used for flavour. Bacon is similar to <a href="/wiki/Salt_pork" title="Salt pork">salt pork</a>, which in modern times is often prepared from similar cuts, but salt pork is never smoked, and has a much higher salt content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebaconpage_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-thebaconpage-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>For safety, bacon may be treated to prevent <a href="/wiki/Trichinosis" title="Trichinosis">trichinosis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fsis_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-fsis-8">[8]</a></sup> caused by <i><a href="/wiki/Trichinella" title="Trichinella">Trichinella</a></i>, a parasitic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roundworm" title="Roundworm">roundworm</a> which can be destroyed by heating, freezing, drying, or smoking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Sodium polyphosphates, such as <a href="/wiki/Sodium_triphosphate" title="Sodium triphosphate">sodium triphosphate</a>, may also be added to make the product easier to slice and to reduce spattering when the bacon is pan-fried.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuts">Cuts</span></h2>
<p>Bacon type differs depending on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuts_of_pork" title="Cuts of pork">primal cut</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">pork</a> from which it is prepared,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebaconpage_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-thebaconpage-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Spruce_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-The_Spruce-1">[1]</a></sup> which reflects local preference.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RawBacon.JPG"><img class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2146" data-file-width="3003" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/RawBacon.JPG/220px-RawBacon.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/RawBacon.JPG/330px-RawBacon.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/RawBacon.JPG/440px-RawBacon.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RawBacon.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Uncooked strips of side bacon</div></div></div>
<ul><li><b>Side bacon</b>, sometimes known as "streaky bacon", comes from the <a href="/wiki/Pork_belly" title="Pork belly">pork belly</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebaconpage_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-thebaconpage-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Spruce_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-The_Spruce-1">[1]</a></sup> It has long alternating layers of fat and muscle running parallel to the <a href="/wiki/Pork_rind" title="Pork rind">rind</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebaconpage_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-thebaconpage-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kiwi_cuts_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-kiwi_cuts-10">[10]</a></sup> This is the most common form of bacon in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebaconpage_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-thebaconpage-7">[7]</a></sup>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Pancetta" title="Pancetta">Pancetta</a> is an Italian form of side bacon, sold smoked or unsmoked (<i>aqua</i>). It is generally rolled up into cylinders after curing, and is known for having a strong flavour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebaconpage_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-thebaconpage-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Spruce_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-The_Spruce-1">[1]</a></sup></li></ul></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bacon.JPG"><img class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Bacon.JPG/220px-Bacon.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Bacon.JPG/330px-Bacon.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Bacon.JPG/440px-Bacon.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bacon.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Uncooked back bacon</div></div></div>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Back_bacon" title="Back bacon">Back bacon</a></b> contains meat from the <a href="/wiki/Loin" title="Loin">loin</a> in the middle of the back of the pig.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebaconpage_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-thebaconpage-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> It is a leaner cut, with less fat compared to side bacon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Spruce_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-The_Spruce-1">[1]</a></sup> Most bacon consumed in the United Kingdom and Ireland is back bacon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebaconpage_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-thebaconpage-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dbmc_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-dbmc-12">[12]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Collar bacon</b> is taken from the back of a pig near the head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebaconpage_7-8"><a href="#cite_note-thebaconpage-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Cottage bacon</b> is made from the lean meat from a boneless pork shoulder that is typically tied into an oval shape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebaconpage_7-9"><a href="#cite_note-thebaconpage-7">[7]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jowl_bacon.jpg"><img class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Jowl_bacon.jpg/220px-Jowl_bacon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Jowl_bacon.jpg/330px-Jowl_bacon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Jowl_bacon.jpg/440px-Jowl_bacon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jowl_bacon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sliced jowl bacon</div></div></div>
<ul><li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jowl_bacon" title="Jowl bacon">Jowl bacon</a></b> is cured and smoked cheeks of pork.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guanciale" title="Guanciale">Guanciale</a> is an Italian jowl bacon that is seasoned and dry cured but not smoked.</li></ul>
<p>The inclusion of skin with a cut of bacon, known as the 'bacon rind',<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> varies, though is less common in the English-speaking world.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Around_the_world">Around the world</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia_and_New_Zealand">Australia and New Zealand</span></h3>
<p>The most common form sold is <i>middle bacon</i>, which includes some of the streaky, fatty section of side bacon along with a portion of the loin of back bacon. In response to increasing consumer <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">diet</a>-consciousness, some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supermarkets" title="Supermarkets">supermarkets</a> also offer the loin section only. This is sold as <i>short cut bacon</i> and is usually priced slightly higher than middle bacon. Both varieties are usually available with the rind removed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_bokchoi-snowpea_4266923676--Roast_peameal_bacon.jpg"><img class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1496" data-file-width="2256" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Flickr_bokchoi-snowpea_4266923676--Roast_peameal_bacon.jpg/220px-Flickr_bokchoi-snowpea_4266923676--Roast_peameal_bacon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Flickr_bokchoi-snowpea_4266923676--Roast_peameal_bacon.jpg/330px-Flickr_bokchoi-snowpea_4266923676--Roast_peameal_bacon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Flickr_bokchoi-snowpea_4266923676--Roast_peameal_bacon.jpg/440px-Flickr_bokchoi-snowpea_4266923676--Roast_peameal_bacon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_bokchoi-snowpea_4266923676--Roast_peameal_bacon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roasted peameal bacon with a maple glaze at the St. Lawrence Market in <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>, Ontario, Canada</div></div></div>
<p>In Canada, the term <i>bacon</i> on its own typically refers to side bacon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canadian_Oxford_Dictionary_2004_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canadian_Oxford_Dictionary_2004-17">[17]</a></sup> Canadian-style back bacon is a lean cut from the eye of the pork loin with little surrounding fat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canadian_Oxford_Dictionary_2004_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Canadian_Oxford_Dictionary_2004-17">[17]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peameal_bacon" title="Peameal bacon">Peameal bacon</a> is an unsmoked back bacon, wet-cured and coated in fine-ground cornmeal (historically, it was rolled in ground, dried <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peas" title="Peas">peas</a>);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canadian_Oxford_Dictionary_2004_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Canadian_Oxford_Dictionary_2004-17">[17]</a></sup> it is popular in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ontario" title="Southern Ontario">southern Ontario</a>. Bacon is often eaten in breakfasts, such as with cooked eggs or pancakes. <a href="/wiki/Maple_syrup" title="Maple syrup">Maple syrup</a> is often used as a flavouring while curing bacon in Canada.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Speck-1.jpg"><img class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1623" data-file-width="2424" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Speck-1.jpg/220px-Speck-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Speck-1.jpg/330px-Speck-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Speck-1.jpg/440px-Speck-1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Speck-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>German <i>Speck</i>, a salted, smoked, and roasted pork fatback or belly cut used as a cold cut</div></div></div>
<p>Some of the meanings of bacon overlap with the German-language term <i><a href="/wiki/Speck" title="Speck">Speck</a></i>. Germans use the term <i>bacon</i> explicitly for <i>Frühstücksspeck</i> ('breakfast <i>Speck</i>') which are cured or smoked pork slices. Traditional German <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cold_cut" title="Cold cut">cold cuts</a> favor ham over bacon, however <i>Wammerl</i> (grilled pork belly) remains popular in Bavaria.
</p><p>Small bacon cubes (called <i>Grieben</i> or <i>Grammerln</i> in Austria and southern Germany) have been a rather important ingredient of various southern German dishes. They are used for adding flavour to soups and salads and for Speck dumplings and various noodle and potato dishes. Instead of preparing them at home from larger slices, they have been sold ready made as <a href="/wiki/Convenience_food" title="Convenience food">convenience foods</a> recently as <i>Baconwürfel</i> ("bacon cubes") in German retail stores.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span></h3>
<p>In Japan, bacon (ベーコン)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> is pronounced "bēkon". It is cured and smoked belly meat as in the US, and is sold in either regular or half-length sizes. Bacon in Japan is different from that in the US in that the meat is not sold raw, but is processed, precooked and has a ham-like consistency when cooked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Uncured, sliced pork belly, known as bara (バラ), is very popular in Japan and is used in a variety of dishes (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Yakitori#Common_non-poultry_dishes" title="Yakitori">yakitori</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yakiniku#Typical_ingredients" title="Yakiniku">yakiniku</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom_and_Ireland">United Kingdom and Ireland</span></h3>
<p>Back bacon is the most common form in the UK and Ireland, and is the usual meaning of the plain term "bacon". A thin slice of bacon is known as a <i>rasher</i>; about 70% of bacon is sold as rashers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Heavily trimmed back cuts which consist of just the eye of meat, known as a <i>medallion</i>, are also available. All types may be unsmoked or smoked. The side cut normal in America is known as "streaky bacon",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> and there is also a long cut, curving round on itself, known as "middle bacon", which is back bacon at one end, and streaky at the other, as well as less common cuts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Bacon is also sold and served as joints, usually boiled, broiled or roast,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> or in thicker slices called chops or steaks. These are usually eaten as part of other meals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jwb_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-jwb-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacon may be cured in several ways, and may be smoked or unsmoked; unsmoked bacon is known as "green bacon".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jwb_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-jwb-5">[5]</a></sup> Fried or grilled bacon rashers are included in the "traditional" <a href="/wiki/Full_breakfast" title="Full breakfast">full breakfast</a>. Hot <a href="/wiki/Bacon_sandwich" title="Bacon sandwich">bacon sandwiches</a> are a popular cafe dish in the UK and Ireland,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cloake20120307_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-cloake20120307-24">[24]</a></sup> and are anecdotally recommended as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hangover_cure" title="Hangover cure">hangover cure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacon is often served with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(food)" title="Egg (food)">eggs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sausages" title="Sausages">sausages</a> as part of a <a href="/wiki/Full_breakfast" title="Full breakfast">full English breakfast</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ebs_full_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-ebs_full-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h3>
<p>The term <i>bacon</i> on its own generally refers to side bacon, which is the most popular type of bacon sold in the US. Back bacon is known as "Canadian bacon" or "Canadian-style bacon", and is usually sold pre-cooked and thick-sliced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kitchen_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-kitchen-27">[27]</a></sup> American bacons include varieties smoked with <a href="/wiki/Hickory" title="Hickory">hickory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesquite" title="Mesquite">mesquite</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apple_tree" title="Apple tree">applewood</a> and flavourings such as <a href="/wiki/Maple" title="Maple">maple</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brown_sugar" title="Brown sugar">brown sugar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Molasses" title="Molasses">molasses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smoky_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-smoky-28">[28]</a></sup> A side of unsliced bacon is known as "slab bacon".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USDA" title="USDA">USDA</a> regulations only recognized bacon as "cured" if it has been treated with synthetic nitrites or nitrates (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Sodium_nitrate" title="Sodium nitrate">sodium nitrate</a> or <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">potassium nitrate</a>). This means that bacon cured with nitrites derived from celery or beets (which has the same chemical outcome) must be labelled "uncured" and include a notice such as "no nitrates or nitrites added except for that naturally occurring in celery". There is also bacon for sale uncured with any nitrites from any sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<p>Salted pork belly first appeared in China. In <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> the term bacon or bacoun referred to all pork in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>  Before the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, bacon was generally produced on local farms and in domestic kitchens. The world's first commercial bacon processing plant was opened in Wiltshire in the 1770s by John Harris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacon_mania">Bacon mania</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bacon_mania" title="Bacon mania">Bacon mania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ChocolateCoveredBaconStick.jpg"><img class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/ChocolateCoveredBaconStick.jpg/220px-ChocolateCoveredBaconStick.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/ChocolateCoveredBaconStick.jpg/330px-ChocolateCoveredBaconStick.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/ChocolateCoveredBaconStick.jpg/440px-ChocolateCoveredBaconStick.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ChocolateCoveredBaconStick.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chocolate-covered bacon on a stick</div></div></div>
<p>The United States and Canada have seen an increase in the popularity of bacon and bacon-related recipes, dubbed "<a href="/wiki/Bacon_mania" title="Bacon mania">bacon mania</a>". The sale of bacon in the US has increased significantly since 2011. Sales climbed 9.5% in 2013, making it an all-time high of nearly $4 billion in US. In a survey conducted by Smithfield, 65% of Americans would support bacon as their "national food".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Passy-US_sales_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Passy-US_sales-33">[33]</a></sup> Dishes such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacon_explosion" title="Bacon explosion">bacon explosion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicken_fried_bacon" title="Chicken fried bacon">chicken fried bacon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chocolate-covered_bacon" title="Chocolate-covered bacon">chocolate-covered bacon</a> have been popularised over the Internet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> as has the use of candied bacon. Recipes spread quickly through both countries' national <a href="/wiki/Media_(communication)" title="Media (communication)">media</a>, culinary <a href="/wiki/Blog" title="Blog">blogs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cook_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-cook-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Restaurants have organised and are organising bacon and <a href="/wiki/Beer_tasting" title="Beer tasting">beer tasting</a> nights,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> reported on bacon infused with <a href="/wiki/Irish_whiskey" title="Irish whiskey">Irish whiskey</a> used for <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick%27s_Day" title="Saint Patrick's Day">Saint Patrick's Day</a> <a href="/wiki/Cocktail" title="Cocktail">cocktails</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> and celebrity chef <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Flay" title="Bobby Flay">Bobby Flay</a> has endorsed a "Bacon of the Month" club online, in print,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> and on national <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Everything_Tastes_Better_with_Bacon" title="Everything Tastes Better with Bacon">Everything Tastes Better with Bacon</a></i>, a 2002 book by Sara Perry, is a cookbook in which all dishes contain bacon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Commentators explain this surging interest in bacon by reference to what they deem <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_culture" title="American culture">American cultural characteristics</a>. Sarah Hepola, in a 2008 article in <i><a href="/wiki/Salon.com" title="Salon.com">Salon.com</a></i>, suggests a number of reasons, one of them being that eating bacon in the modern, health-conscious world is an act of rebellion: "Loving bacon is like shoving a middle finger in the face of all that is healthy and holy while an unfiltered cigarette smoulders between your lips."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bacon_mania_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-bacon_mania-42">[42]</a></sup> She also suggests bacon is sexy (with a reference to Sarah Katherine Lewis' book <i>Sex and Bacon</i>), kitsch, and funny. Hepola concludes by saying that "Bacon is American".
</p><p>Alison Cook, writing in the <i><a href="/wiki/Houston_Chronicle" title="Houston Chronicle">Houston Chronicle</a></i>, argues the case of bacon's American citizenship by referring to historical and geographical uses of bacon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cook_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-cook-35">[35]</a></sup> Early American literature echoes the sentiment—in <a href="/wiki/Ebenezer_Cooke_(poet)" title="Ebenezer Cooke (poet)">Ebenezer Cooke</a>'s 1708 poem <i>The Sot-Weed Factor</i>, a satire of life in early colonial America, the narrator already complains that practically all the food in America was bacon-infused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of December 2016, the U.S. national frozen pork belly inventory totaled 17.8 million lb (8.1 million kg), the lowest level in 50 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacon_dishes">Bacon dishes</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_bacon_dishes" title="List of bacon dishes">List of bacon dishes</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mmm..._BLT_for_me!_(5487341380).jpg"><img class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1135" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Mmm..._BLT_for_me%21_%285487341380%29.jpg/220px-Mmm..._BLT_for_me%21_%285487341380%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Mmm..._BLT_for_me%21_%285487341380%29.jpg/330px-Mmm..._BLT_for_me%21_%285487341380%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Mmm..._BLT_for_me%21_%285487341380%29.jpg/440px-Mmm..._BLT_for_me%21_%285487341380%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mmm..._BLT_for_me!_(5487341380).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/BLT" title="BLT">bacon, lettuce, and tomato (BLT)</a> sandwich</div></div></div>
<p>Bacon dishes include bacon and eggs, <a href="/wiki/BLT" title="BLT">bacon, lettuce, and tomato (BLT)</a> sandwiches, <a href="/wiki/Cobb_salad" title="Cobb salad">Cobb salad</a>, and various bacon-wrapped foods, such as <a href="/wiki/Scallop" title="Scallop">scallops</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimp</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Asparagus" title="Asparagus">asparagus</a>. Recently invented bacon dishes include <a href="/wiki/Chicken_fried_bacon" title="Chicken fried bacon">chicken fried bacon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chocolate_covered_bacon" title="Chocolate covered bacon">chocolate covered bacon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bacon_jerky" title="Bacon jerky">bacon jerky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bacon_ice_cream" title="Bacon ice cream">bacon ice cream</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacon_explosion" title="Bacon explosion">bacon explosion</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tatws_Pum_Munud" title="Tatws Pum Munud">Tatws Pum Munud</a> is a traditional Welsh stew, made with sliced potatoes, vegetables and smoked bacon. <a href="/wiki/Bacon_jam" title="Bacon jam">Bacon jam</a> and bacon marmalade are also commercially available.
</p><p>Streaky bacon is more commonly used as a topping in the US on such items as <a href="/wiki/Pizza" title="Pizza">pizza</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salads" title="Salads">salads</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sandwiches" title="Sandwiches">sandwiches</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hamburgers" title="Hamburgers">hamburgers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baked_potatoes" title="Baked potatoes">baked potatoes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hot_dogs" title="Hot dogs">hot dogs</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soups" title="Soups">soups</a>. In the US, sliced smoked back bacon is used less frequently than the streaky variety, but can sometimes be found on pizza, salads, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omelettes" title="Omelettes">omelettes</a>.
</p><p>Bacon is also used as an accent in dishes, such as bacon-topped <a href="/wiki/Meatloaf" title="Meatloaf">meatloaf</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Saut%C3%A9ing" title="Sautéing">sautéed</a> crisp and crumbled into <a href="/wiki/Green_bean" title="Green bean">green beans</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> or as a crumble in a salad.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacon_bits" title="Bacon bits">Bacon bits</a> are crumbled bacon in <a href="/wiki/Condiment" title="Condiment">condiment</a> form, typically commercially prepared and preserved for long <a href="/wiki/Shelf_life" title="Shelf life">shelf life</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacon_fat">Bacon fat</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_cyclonebill_-_Bacon_(1).jpg"><img class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Flickr_-_cyclonebill_-_Bacon_%281%29.jpg/220px-Flickr_-_cyclonebill_-_Bacon_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Flickr_-_cyclonebill_-_Bacon_%281%29.jpg/330px-Flickr_-_cyclonebill_-_Bacon_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Flickr_-_cyclonebill_-_Bacon_%281%29.jpg/440px-Flickr_-_cyclonebill_-_Bacon_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_cyclonebill_-_Bacon_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bacon frying in its own grease</div></div></div>
<p>Bacon fat liquefies and becomes <a href="/wiki/Dripping" title="Dripping">drippings</a> when it is heated. Once cool, it firms into a form of <a href="/wiki/Lard" title="Lard">lard</a>. Bacon fat is flavourful and is used for various cooking purposes. Traditionally, bacon grease is saved in British and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_U.S._cuisine" title="Southern U.S. cuisine">southern US cuisine</a>, and used as a base for cooking and as an all-purpose flavouring, for everything from <a href="/wiki/Gravy" title="Gravy">gravy</a> to <a href="/wiki/Cornbread" title="Cornbread">cornbread</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cornbread_Gravy_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cornbread_Gravy-50">[50]</a></sup> to <a href="/wiki/Salad_dressing" title="Salad dressing">salad dressing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bacon_Vinaigrette_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bacon_Vinaigrette-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Griebenschmalz-1.jpg"><img class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1230" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Griebenschmalz-1.jpg/220px-Griebenschmalz-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Griebenschmalz-1.jpg/330px-Griebenschmalz-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Griebenschmalz-1.jpg/440px-Griebenschmalz-1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Griebenschmalz-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>German Griebenschmalz used as spread</div></div></div>
<p>In Germany, <i>Griebenschmalz</i> is a popular spread made from bacon lard.
</p><p>Bacon is often used for a cooking technique called <i>barding</i> consisting of laying or wrapping strips of bacon or other fats over a roast to provide additional fat to a lean piece of meat. It is often used for roast <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game_(food)" title="Game (food)">game birds</a>, and is a traditional method of preparing beef <a href="/wiki/Filet_mignon" title="Filet mignon">filet mignon</a>, which is wrapped in strips of bacon before cooking. The bacon itself may afterwards be discarded or served to eat, like <a href="/wiki/Pork_rind" title="Pork rind">cracklings</a>. It may also be cut into <a href="/wiki/Lardon" title="Lardon">lardons</a>.
</p><p>One teaspoon (4 g or 0.14 oz) of bacon grease has 38 calories (40 kJ/g).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nutrition_Data_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nutrition_Data-52">[52]</a></sup> It is composed almost completely of fat, with very little additional nutritional value. Bacon fat is roughly 40% <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nutrition_Data_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nutrition_Data-52">[52]</a></sup> Despite the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturated_fat_and_cardiovascular_disease_controversy" title="Saturated fat and cardiovascular disease controversy">disputed health risks</a> of excessive bacon grease consumption, it remains popular in the cuisine of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_South" title="American South">American South</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrients">Nutrients</span></h2>
<p>One 10-g slice of cooked side bacon contains 4.5 g of fat, 3.0 g of protein, and 205 mg of sodium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The fat, protein, and sodium content varies depending on the cut and cooking method.
</p><p>68% of the food energy of bacon comes from fat, almost half of which is saturated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-webmd_health_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-webmd_health-55">[55]</a></sup> A serving of three slices of bacon contains 30 milligrams of <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> (0.1%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-webmd_health_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-webmd_health-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffpost_baconfacts_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-huffpost_baconfacts-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_concerns">Health concerns</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dietary_recommendations_for_saturated_fat" title="Dietary recommendations for saturated fat">Dietary recommendations for saturated fat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Health_effects_of_salt" title="Health effects of salt">Health effects of salt</a></div>
<p>Studies have consistently found the consumption of processed meat to be linked to increased <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">mortality</a>, and to an increased risk of developing a number of serious health conditions including <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular disease</a> and <a href="/wiki/Type_2_diabetes" title="Type 2 diabetes">type 2 diabetes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-villain_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-villain-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolk_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-wolk-58">[58]</a></sup> Although as of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bacon&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> these links have not been definitely established as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Causal" title="Causal">causal</a>, they are likely to be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolk_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-wolk-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacon can contain nitrites, which can form carcinogenic <a href="/wiki/Nitroso" title="Nitroso">nitroso</a>-compounds such as <a href="/wiki/S-Nitrosothiol" title="S-Nitrosothiol">S-Nitrosothiols</a> , nitrosyl-<a href="/wiki/Heme" title="Heme">heme</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrosamine" title="Nitrosamine">nitrosamines</a>. In the United States, <a href="/wiki/Sodium_nitrite" title="Sodium nitrite">sodium nitrite</a> cannot exceed certain levels in bacon. <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">Vitamin C</a> (ascorbate) or <a href="/wiki/Sodium_erythorbate" title="Sodium erythorbate">sodium erythorbate</a> can be added to bacon, which greatly reduces the formation of nitrosamines but has no effect on <a href="/wiki/S-Nitrosothiol" title="S-Nitrosothiol">S-Nitrosothiols</a> and nitrosyl-<a href="/wiki/Heme" title="Heme">heme</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_E" title="Vitamin E">Vitamin E</a> (tocopherol) also reduces nitrosamine levels. Bacon fried at higher temperatures potentially has more nitrosamines than bacon fried at lower temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> in 2015, regular consumption of processed meats such as bacon increases the likelihood of developing <a href="/wiki/Colorectal_cancer" title="Colorectal cancer">colorectal cancers</a> by 18%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternatives">Alternatives</span></h2>
<p>Several alternatives to and substitutes for bacon have been developed for those who cannot or prefer not to eat standard pork bacon, including beef, chicken, turkey, bison, soy, and coconut bacon.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Turkey_bacon">Turkey bacon</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Turkey_bacon" title="Turkey bacon">Turkey bacon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Turkey_bacon_cooking_in_skillet.jpg"><img class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Turkey_bacon_cooking_in_skillet.jpg/220px-Turkey_bacon_cooking_in_skillet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Turkey_bacon_cooking_in_skillet.jpg/330px-Turkey_bacon_cooking_in_skillet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Turkey_bacon_cooking_in_skillet.jpg/440px-Turkey_bacon_cooking_in_skillet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Turkey_bacon_cooking_in_skillet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chopped, formed, colored, and flavored turkey bacon strips</div></div></div>
<p>Turkey bacon is consumed by some as an alternative to pork bacon for health benefits, religious laws, or other concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Spruce_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-The_Spruce-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfgate_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-sfgate-62">[62]</a></sup> It is lower in fat and food energy than bacon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Spruce_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-The_Spruce-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfgate_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-sfgate-62">[62]</a></sup> but is used similarly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfgate_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-sfgate-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>The meat for turkey bacon comes from the whole turkey, which is chopped and reformed into strips to resemble bacon, and can be cured or uncured, or <a href="/wiki/Smoking_(cooking)" title="Smoking (cooking)">smoked</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Spruce_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-The_Spruce-1">[1]</a></sup> Turkey bacon is cooked by pan-frying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfgate_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-sfgate-62">[62]</a></sup> Cured turkey bacon made from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dark_meat" title="Dark meat">dark meat</a> can be less than 10% fat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-support_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-support-63">[63]</a></sup> The low fat content of turkey bacon means it does not shrink while being cooked and has a tendency to stick to the pan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-support_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-support-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Macon">Macon</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Macon_(food)" title="Macon (food)">Macon (food)</a></div>
<p>"Macon" is produced by curing cuts of <a href="/wiki/Lamb_and_mutton" title="Lamb and mutton">mutton</a> in a manner similar to the production of pork bacon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Historically produced in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, it was introduced across Britain during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> as a consequence of rationing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time_note_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-time_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orwell_note_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-orwell_note-66">[66]</a></sup> It is today available as an alternative to bacon, produced for the Muslim market and sold at <a href="/wiki/Halal" title="Halal">halal</a> butchers; it is largely similar in appearance to pork bacon except for the darker colour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vegetarian_bacon">Vegetarian bacon</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vegetarian_bacon" title="Vegetarian bacon">Vegetarian bacon</a></div>
<p>Vegetarian bacon, also referred to as <i>facon</i>, <i>veggie bacon</i>, or <i>vacon</i>, is a vegetarian "bacon" made from plant matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bacon.co.uk_veggie_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-bacon.co.uk_veggie-68">[68]</a></sup> It has no cholesterol, is low in fat, and contains large amounts of protein and fibre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bacon.co.uk_veggie_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-bacon.co.uk_veggie-68">[68]</a></sup> Two slices contain about 310 kilojoules (74 kcal).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bacon.co.uk_veggie_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-bacon.co.uk_veggie-68">[68]</a></sup> Vegetarian bacon is usually made from marinated strips of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Textured_soy_protein" title="Textured soy protein">textured soy protein</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">tempeh</a> (fermented soybeans).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacon-flavoured_products">Bacon-flavoured products</span></h2>
<p>The popularity of bacon in the United States has given rise to a number of commercial products that promise to add bacon flavouring without the labour involved in cooking it.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacon_bits">Bacon bits</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bacon_bits_in_a_bowl_(cropped).jpg"><img class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2283" data-file-width="2455" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Bacon_bits_in_a_bowl_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Bacon_bits_in_a_bowl_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Bacon_bits_in_a_bowl_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Bacon_bits_in_a_bowl_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Bacon_bits_in_a_bowl_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Bacon_bits_in_a_bowl_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bacon_bits_in_a_bowl_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bacon bits in a bowl</div></div></div>
<p>Bacon bits are a frequently used topping on <a href="/wiki/Salad" title="Salad">salad</a> or potatoes, and a common element of <a href="/wiki/Salad_bar" title="Salad bar">salad bars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Spruce_1-10"><a href="#cite_note-The_Spruce-1">[1]</a></sup> They are usually salted. Bacon bits are made from small, crumbled pieces of bacon;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Spruce_1-11"><a href="#cite_note-The_Spruce-1">[1]</a></sup> in commercial plants they are cooked in continuous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microwave_ovens" title="Microwave ovens">microwave ovens</a>. Similar products are made from ham or turkey, and analogues are made from <a href="/wiki/Textured_vegetable_protein" title="Textured vegetable protein">textured vegetable protein</a>, artificially flavoured to resemble bacon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_bacon-flavoured_products">Other bacon-flavoured products</span></h3>
<p>There is also a wide range of other bacon-flavoured products, including a bacon-flavoured salt (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacon_Salt" title="Bacon Salt">Bacon Salt</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Spruce_1-12"><a href="#cite_note-The_Spruce-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abcnews_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-abcnews-70">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baconnaise" title="Baconnaise">Baconnaise</a> (a bacon-flavoured mayonnaise),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Spruce_1-13"><a href="#cite_note-The_Spruce-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bacon_Grill" title="Bacon Grill">Bacon Grill</a> (a tinned meat, similar to <a href="/wiki/Spam_(food)" title="Spam (food)">Spam</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Bacon_ice_cream" title="Bacon ice cream">bacon ice cream</a>.
</p>
